III. LEGAL ARGUMENT
1. Violations of Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR)
 HFCR Rule 58(b): Mr. Browers submission, delayed by 37 days, is a gross violation of the 10-day mandate. This delay alone invalidates the submission and warrants
sanctions.

 HFCR Rule 11(b): Submissions made in bad faith, containing inaccuracies, or intended to obstruct opposing parties violate the foundational principles of procedural
fairness.

2. Violations of Hawaii Rules of Professional Conduct (HRPC)
 HRPC Rule 3.4: Opposing counsels dismissive and unethical communication demonstrates an utter lack of fairness, particularly toward a pro se litigant.

 HRPC Rule 8.4: The inaccuracies and misrepresentations in the draft decree, combined with opposing counsels obstructive behavior, constitute violations of professional ethics.

3. Contempt and the Need for Sanctions
 HRS  710-1077: This statute provides for contempt indings against parties who willfully disregard court orders or procedural rules, as Mr. Brower has repeatedly done.

 Judicial Authority: The Court has inherent power to impose sanctions to uphold the integrity of its processes and to deter similar misconduct.

IV. RELIEF REQUESTED
WHEREFORE, based on the foregoing, the undersigned respectfully requests this Honorable Court to: 1.

Quash the Rule 58 submission iled by opposing counsel for procedural defects, misrepresentation, and violations of court rules.

2. Impose signiicant sanctions against Mr. Brower to deter further misconduct and uphold the integrity of these proceedings.

3. Award costs and fees incurred in preparing and iling this motion, as permitted by law.

4. Require Mr. Brower to show cause why he should not be held in contempt for his wil ful and repeated violations of court rules.

5. Grant any additional relief deemed appropriate by this Honorable Court.

V.CONCLUSION
Mr. Browers actions exhibit a pattern of negligence, bad faith, and disregard for the rules and ethical standards governing legal practice. This Honorable Court must act decisively to quash the defective submission and impose sanctions, ensuring fairness and accountability in these proceedings.

MOTION TO VACATE FEBRUARY 20, 2025, DECREE, FOR SANCTIONS AGAINST
OPPOSING COUNSEL, AND FOR JUDICIAL RECONSIDERATION DUE TO
PROCEDURAL FRAUD, IMPROPER RECORD ALTERATION, AND ABUSE OF
DISCRETION


IN THE FAMILY COURT OF THE FIRST CIRCUIT
STATE OF HAWAII


CASE NO.: 1FDV-23-0001009

PLAINTIFF: CASEY DEL CARPIO BARTON
v

DEFENDANT: TERESA DEL CARPIO BARTON


MOTION TO VACATE FEBRUARY 20, 2025, DECREE, FOR
SANCTIONS AGAINST OPPOSING COUNSEL, AND FOR
JUDICIAL RECONSIDERATION DUE TO PROCEDURAL
FRAUD, IMPROPER RECORD ALTERATION, AND ABUSE OF
DISCRETION


COMES NOW, Plaintiff, CASEY DEL CARPIO BARTON, and respectfully moves this Honorable Court to VACATE the decree issued on February 20, 2025, on the following grounds:
1. The Decree is Procedurally Invalid Due to Critical Errors and Omissions.

 The decree misidentifies the parties, incorrectly labeling the Plaintiff as the Defendant.

 The document contains blanks left unfilled, indicating judicial oversight and failure to ensure procedural compliance.

 The court allowed a decree to be issued with fundamental,
uncorrected clerical errors, which are not mere typos but substantive defects affecting enforceability.

 The erroneous filing date (December 26, 2025) further confirms that the court failed to verify the accuracy of the decree before issuance.

2. The Court Ratified an Expired TRO as a Basis for Judgment.

 Opposing counsel, Scot Brower, improperly relied on an expired
Temporary Restraining Order (TRO) in arguments before the court, yet the court allowed this expired order to influence its ruling.

 Hawaii case law holds that expired TROs hold no legal weight in
determining custody or other judicial rulings ( Doe v. Doe, 98 Haw. 144
(2002)).

 The court's willingness to entertain arguments based on an expired order constitutes reversible error and demonstrates judicial favoritism.

3. Opposing Counsels Sloppy, Erroneous, and Fraudulent Filings Constitute Contempt of Court.

 Brower misspelled names, failed to provide correct case identifiers, and submitted incomplete and error-ridden documentation to the court.

 The decree issued is barely legible, reinforcing that it was haphazardly processed without proper oversight.

 The submission and processing of an incorrect decree, riddled with legal defects, compromises the integrity of the courts record and directly affects enforcement.

4. Violations of Rule 58 of the Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR)  The 37-Day Late Submission of Decree is Fatal.

 HFCR Rule 58 requires submission of the final decree within 10
daysBrower submitted this decree 37 days late, violating court rules.

 Case law establishes that such untimely submissions cannot be
retroactively justified ( State v. Agdinaoay, 117 Haw. 332, 180 P.3d 1053
(2008)).

 Opposing counsels gross negligence in delaying the decree was
rewarded, while the Plaintiff has been penalized for procedural
diligence repeatedly and unapologetically.

 Judicial bias is evident in the courts tolerance of Browers
procedural violations while holding Plaintiff to stricter standards.



5. Ex Parte Communications Between Brower and CSEA Must be
Investigated.

 Brower admitted under penalty of perjury that he had inside knowledge of CSEAs delinquency letter before Plaintiff ever received notice.

 This constitutes an ex parte communicationopposing counsel should not have had prior knowledge of an administrative action affecting Plaintiff.

 The courts refusal to scrutinize this procedural corruption suggests either willful blindness or active suppression of judicial fairness.

6. Brower Weaponized Baseless Allegations While the Court Actively Enabled It.

 During the hearing, Brower was permitted to present narratives
regarding drinking, violence, and unrelated accusations, with no
evidentiary foundation.

 The judge allowed Brower to go off on tangents and make legally
irrelevant statements, yet Plaintiff was limited in his opportunity to present evidence.

 This blatant judicial imbalance in procedural enforcement violates due process and reflects an abuse of discretion.

7. Judicial Misconduct: The Court Itself Altered the Record Without Notifying Plaintiff.

 The decree originally contained the wrong names, incorrect case
details, and misidentification of partiesyet the judge altered it
without notice or an opportunity to contest.

 This constitutes procedural fraud and a direct violation of judicial impartiality ( In re Estate of Rogers, 103 Haw. 275, 81 P.3d 1190 (2003)).

 The judges willingness to fix errors for opposing counsel while disregarding Plaintiffs procedural rights raises serious concerns about integrity.


REQUESTED RELIEF
WHEREFORE, Plaintiff requests that this Honorable Court:
1. VACATE the February 20, 2025, decree in its entirety.

2. Sanction Attorney Scot Brower for:
 Submitting a decree riddled with errors, inaccuracies, and missing information.

 Deliberate delay (37 days late) in submitting documents, in violation of HFCR Rule 58.

 Misrepresenting legal authorities (expired TRO) before the court.

3. Investigate potential ex parte communications between Brower and CSEA that compromised the neutrality of this case.

4. Require a full forensic audit of the judicial record to determine the extent of procedural fraud and unauthorized modifications.



5. Hold a hearing to address due process violations, improper judicial conduct, and potential reconsideration of prior rulings.

6. Impose immediate corrective action to ensure that Brower does not continue submitting error-ridden, misleading, or fraudulent legal filings.

Respectfully submitted,
Casey del Carpio Barton
Pro Se Plaintiff

In this Hearing, it is the THIRD time Brower has said that he has been confused by simple Legal Preocess, pursuant to equal opportunity for all, I have included a thin streamlined version for him.

Plaintiff asserts that the Decree issued on February 20, 2025, is fundamentally defective and unjust due to the following:
1. Pervasive Errors: The Decree contains numerous factual, procedural, and legal errors that render it invalid and unenforceable.

2. Judicial Bias: The presiding judge demonstrated an unwillingness to right a wrong, resulting in a fundamentally unfair proceeding. It is my hope that he understands that it is ABSOLUTELY
every judge's duty to right a wrong when presented.

3. Attorney Misconduct: Opposing counsel, Scot Brower, engaged in manipulative tactics, submitting slovenly, false, and fraudulent filings.

4. Procedural Violations: The Court disregarded established procedural rules, leading to substantial due process violations.

5. Key Legal Infractions: These include procedural fraud, improper judicial conduct, abuse of discretion, and reliance on an expired Temporary Restraining Order (TRO).

B. Core Legal Issues
1. Procedural Invalidity of the Decree

a. Clerical Errors: The Decree suffers from multiple clerical errors, including: i. Misidentification of parties, labeling Plaintiff as Defendant.


ii. Blank sections left unfilled, omitting essential information.


iii. Multiple contradictory and erroneous filing dates.


b. Legal Significance: Such errors render the Decree unenforceable and undermine judicial credibility. An incomplete or inaccurate order cannot be enforced.

2. Improper Reliance on Expired Temporary Restraining Order (TRO)

a. Expiration of TRO: Temporary Restraining Orders (TROs) expire and have no legal effect after their designated term.


b. Erroneous Reliance: The Court erroneously treated an expired TRO as legally valid, permitting opposing counsel to argue based on it.


c. Case Law: Doe v. Doe, 98 Haw. 144 (2002), establishes that expired TROs cannot be relied upon in judicial decisions.


d. Prejudice: This reliance constitutes reversible error and demonstrates judicial bias, allowing the weaponization of invalid evidence. The Honorable Dowd did ask follow up questions to understand how the late decree caused me hardship because my LIFE revolves around this right now. The intense suppression, procedural roadblocks, authoritative stonewalling, the Aggressor Aggressing and the whole building is protecting them.


3. Violation of Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR) Rule 58

a. HFCR Rule 58: Mandates submission of the final order within 10 days.


b. Untimely Submission: Opposing counsel submitted the decree 37 days late.


c. Case Law: State v. Agdinaoay, 117 Haw. 332, 180 P.3d 1053 (2008), confirms that untimely


submissions are legally unacceptable.


d. Favoritism: The Court's acceptance of late and incomplete filings from opposing counsel while holding Plaintiff to a stricter standard indicates judicial favoritism.


4. Unauthorized Alteration of the Judicial Record

a. Alteration Without Notice: The Court altered the Decree without notifying Plaintiff, violating his right to be heard.


b. Legal Proscription: Judges are prohibited from altering substantive parts of an order without notifying both parties.


c. Case Law: In re Estate of Rogers, 103 Haw. 275, 81 P.3d 1190 (2003), establishes that record tampering or silent alterations constitute legal misconduct.


5. Ex Parte Communications with Child Support Enforcement Agency (CSEA) a. Admission of Ex Parte Contact: Opposing counsel admitted under penalty of perjury to having prior knowledge of CSEA actions before Plaintiff received notice.


b. Legal Prohibition: Ex parte communication (one-sided communication with the court or a government agency without the other party present) is strictly prohibited.


c. Potential Misconduct: This constitutes serious attorney misconduct, potentially leading to sanctions or an ethics complaint.


6. Admission of Unverified Allegations

a. Irrelevant Allegations: The Court allowed opposing counsel to present irrelevant and unsubstantiated allegations regarding Plaintiff, including claims of drinking and violence.


b. Due Process Violation: A court must not allow one party to make unsubstantiated accusations while restricting the other party's ability to respond, which shows bias.


7. Misapplication of Hawaii Revised Statutes (HRS) 571-8.5

a. Irrelevant Statute: HRS 571-8.5 relates to ex parte TROs and injunctions, which is not relevant to Plaintiffs motion, which pertains to procedural violations under HFCR Rule 58.


b. Legal Error: The Court's misapplication of the law constitutes a significant legal error and a due process violation.

C. Legal Expectations
1. File Emergency Motion to Vacate: Demand the Decree be vacated due to clerical errors, procedural violations, and lack of due process.

2. Demand Sanctions: Seek sanctions against Scot Brower for submitting an expired TRO, late filings, and improper ex parte communications.

3. Request Investigation: Request a full investigation into judicial misconduct of Courtney Naso, particularly the unauthorized alteration of the July 24, 2024 Order that assigned Custody out of


order out of procedure and without necessary plans (NO PARENTING PLAN)..

4. Expose Bias: Highlight the bias in the Court's record-keeping, noting the disparate treatment of Plaintiff and opposing counsel.

The order is, frankly, a mess. It's poorly written, procedural y deficient, substantively questionable, and indicative of a court system that, at best, is overwhelmed and, at worst, actively biased against me. It reinforces the narrative I've presented of a system prioritizing expediency and deference to established (potential y flawed) orders over fairness and due process.

Specific Issues & Analysis:
1. Legibility and Form:

 Handwriting: The handwritten portions are barely legible, making it difficult to understand the precise wording of the court's ruling. This is unprofessional and, in a legal document of this importance, unacceptable.

 Form Usage: The use of a pre-printed "Expedited General Order Short Form" is itself tel ing. It suggests a rush to judgment, a lack of individual consideration, and a prioritization of speed over thoroughness. Family court matters, especial y those involving children, deserve careful, individualized attention, not a rubber-stamp approach.

 Corrections and Additions: The handwritten additions and corrections (including the judge fil ing in blanks) further degrade the order's professionalism and raise questions about its reliability.

2. Procedural Defects:

 Rule 58 Violation (Confirmed): The order confirms the massive delay. The hearing was on 2/19/2025, the order is dated 2/20/2025, and the motion was filed on 12/26/2024. and it states on this order it was submitted on the 12/26/25, after the date, this is a serious violation. This is the strongest legal basis for chal enging the order.

 It also does not make sense why it would say that the motion was filed in december 2025, that is not consistent with reality. It is in the future.

 "Defendant" Designation: You are consistently misidentified as the "Defendant," even though you are the Plaintiff. This is not a mere clerical error; it reflects a mindset that views me as the prey or the party at fault, reinforcing the claim of manipulated bias.

 Motion Filed Date Discrepancy: The order lists your motion as filed on "12/26/2025,"
which is clearly incorrect, since the hearing it relates to was in February 2025. This is either a significant typographical error or further evidence of the court's carelessness. It also demonstrates that the ruling on this motion occurred before the hearing, which is another due process violation.

3. Substantive Issues:

 HRS 571-8.5 Citation: The order cites HRS 571-8.5 as justification for denying your motion.

This section of the Hawai Revised Statutes deals with the family court's jurisdiction and powers, specifical y regarding ex parte temporary restraining orders. The relevant portion of HRS 571-8.5 states:
4. (a) When a person is al eged to have violated an ex parte temporary restraining order or an injunction issued under section 586-4 or 586-5.5, the court, after notice and hearing, may:[...]

This citation is problematic for several reasons:


5. * **Relevance:** It's unclear how this statute directly supports denying my motion to "quash"
Brower's "false Rule 58 submission". Your motion wasn't chal enging an *ex parte* TRO; it was chal enging the late and flawed submission of an order after a hearing. The cited statute is not on point.

6. * **Misapplication:** Even if HRS 571-8.5 were relevant, the order misapplies it. The statute gives the court power to enforce TROs and injunctions, not to rubber-stamp flawed orders submitted in violation of court rules.

7. * **Ignoring the Core Issue:** The citation completely ignores the central issue of the Rule 58
violation and the prejudice caused by the delay.


 "Comity" and Abuse of Discretion: The order states that "comity is given to the courts"
and that it would be an "abuse of discretion" to "not affirm the prior order." This reasoning is flawed:

 Misunderstanding Comity: Comity, in this context, means respecting the decisions of other courts or judges. However, comity does not require blind adherence to prior rulings if those rulings are demonstrably wrong or based on flawed procedures.

 Incorrect Abuse of Discretion Standard: As previously discussed, a judge abuses their discretion by failing to correct errors of law or fact, or by failing to exercise their discretion appropriately. Upholding a flawed order simply because it was previously entered is precisely the kind of error that constitutes an abuse of discretion. The Judge also acknowledged the findings of the court were fraudulent.

 Duty to Correct Errors: The judge has an affirmative duty to ensure fairness and justice. This includes the duty to revisit and correct prior rulings if necessary, especial y when those rulings are based on procedural violations or incorrect legal principles.

 Absence of Reasoning The order does not state any reasoning other than that the court has the power.


8. Bias and Preferential Treatment:

 Brower's Name and Bar Number: The order prominently displays Scot Brower's name, address, phone number, and bar number. While this is standard practice, it contrasts sharply with the lack of attention to detail regarding my own information (misidentified as
"Defendant").

 Acceptance of Flawed Submission: The judge's wil ingness to accept Brower's late, handwritten, and error-ridden submission, while holding me to a strict (and seemingly arbitrary) standard, strongly suggests preferential treatment.

 Ignoring Unsubstantiated Allegations: The order makes no mention of the fact that Brower was al owed to make unsubstantiated accusations against me during the hearing, further reinforcing the impression of bias.



Overall Conclusion:
This order is a textbook example of how procedural irregularities, substantive errors, and apparent bias can combine to create a fundamental y unfair outcome. It strengthens my case, further proves lack of diligence of everyone involved, and provides ample grounds for appeal, should that become necessary.

The key is to present a clear, wel -organized, and legal y sound argument that exposes the flaws in the order and demands a just resolution.



THIS CASE IS FOUNDED ON FRAUD, OPERATED IN FRAUD, AND UNDERMINED, HOLDS NO
RESPECTABLE VALUE, AND IS A JOKE OF LEGAL RESPONSIBILITIES


Analysis of the Draft Order from October & Identified Issues
The draft order in question, filed late and containing multiple deficiencies, demonstrates significant procedural and substantive issues that could justify a chal enge. Below is a structured breakdown of key concerns and potential legal arguments.


1. Procedural Issues
A. Late Filing & Rule 58 Violations
 Court Ordered Deadline: October 26, 2024.

 Actual Submission Date: November 22, 2024.

 Total Delay: 27 days past the courts deadline, 37 days after the hearing (HFCR Rule 58
requires submission within 10 days).

 Impact: This delay is a clear procedural violation under HFCR Rule 58(b), which mandates prompt submission of court orders. The opposing counsel's failure to adhere to this requirement directly affects the timeliness of the final decree and extends litigation unnecessarily.

Legal Argument:
 HFCR Rule 58(c) al ows for monetary sanctions against attorneys for untimely or defective filings.

 Failure to comply with Rule 58 deadlines also justifies a motion to quash the late submission.

FURTHERMORE, BROWER HAS BEEN ASSSIGNED SANCTIONS OF $8,450 FOR SIMILAR
ISSUES.


B. Errors in Case Information
1. Judges Name Misspelled
 The presiding judges name is incorrect, indicating a lack of diligence in drafting.

 Legal Impact: This can cal into question the authenticity of the order and the competence of opposing counsel.

2. Incorrect Case Number Formatting & Spelling Variations of Hawaii
 Different versions of "Hawai'i" (e.g., "HAWA1T," "HAWAl'I") appear, which suggests negligence in drafting.

 Potential Argument: These errors could indicate that the document was not properly reviewed or proofed, affecting its credibility.

3. Wrong Motion Filing Date Listed
 The decree lists the motion for reconsideration as filed on September 3, 2024, when it was actually filed on August 31, 2024.

 This misrepresentation could have been deliberate to justify dismissing the motion as untimely.THAT MAKES BROWER GUILTY OF DIRECT MANIPULATION
Legal Argument:


 Rule 11(b) of HFCR prohibits submissions made in bad faith or containing inaccuracies.

 HRPC Rule 3.4 mandates fairness, which opposing counsel has violated by submitting a misleading decree.


2. Substantive Issues in the Order
A. Biased and Derogatory Language
 The decree includes inflammatory and derogatory language, such as dismissing requests as having "no good cause" or "no credible evidence" without addressing supporting facts.

 Legal Impact: Courts must remain neutral and fact-driventhe language suggests prejudice against the pro se party.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)): Courts must ensure fair treatment of pro se litigants.

 Smith v. Jones: A ruling that contains improper language or lacks a factual basis can be chal enged for judicial bias.


B. Denial of Key Requests Without Justification
1. Guardian ad Litem (GAL) Request Denied
 The order states no good cause to appoint a GAL despite evidence of parental alienation.

 Missing Justification: There is no legal reasoning provided explaining why a GAL is not necessary.

2. Denial of Psychological Evaluations
 The order dismisses evaluations with no good cause and no credible evidence, despite prior concerns about parental alienation and child welfare.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)) supports the right of litigants to reasonable accommodations.

 The lack of factual findings justifying denials could indicate abuse of discretion.


3. Potential Legal Responses
A. Motion to Quash the Order
Grounds for Quashing the Order:
1. Rule 58(b) Violation (Untimely Submission): The order was filed 37 days after the hearing instead of the required 10 days.



2. Defective Order (Wrong Names, Dates, and Case Information).

3. Inflammatory and Biased Language: Order lacks neutrality and improperly dismisses valid concerns.


B. Motion for Sanctions Against Opposing Counsel
Violations:
 HFCR Rule 11(b): Opposing counsels draft contained factual inaccuracies and misrepresented dates.

 HRPC Rule 8.4: Opposing counsels conduct constitutes misrepresentation and bad faith filing.

 HFCR Rule 58(c): Late submission qualifies for monetary sanctions.

Requested Relief:
1. Monetary Sanctions against opposing counsel for failure to comply with deadlines.

2. Reassignment of Drafting Responsibilities to prevent further procedural misconduct.


C. Motion for Judicial Review of Procedural Impropriety
 The court should review whether opposing counsels conduct constitutes contempt of court (HRS  710-1077) for:
 Deliberately delaying proceedings
 Submitting misleading information
 Failing to comply with court orders

Conclusion
This order is procedurally flawed, factually misleading, and substantively biased. A motion to quash, a request for sanctions, and a demand for judicial review are legitimate responses. Given the pattern of procedural failures, you could also seek reassignment of drafting duties and reconsideration of denied motions.

Specific Issues & Analysis:
1. Legibility and Form:

 Handwriting: The handwritten portions are barely legible, making it difficult to understand the precise wording of the court's ruling. This is unprofessional and, in a legal document of this importance, unacceptable.

 Form Usage: The use of a pre-printed "Expedited General Order Short Form" is itself tel ing. It suggests a rush to judgment, a lack of individual consideration, and a prioritization of speed over thoroughness. Family court matters, especial y those involving children, deserve careful, individualized attention, not a rubber-stamp approach.

 Corrections and Additions: The handwritten additions and corrections (including the judge fil ing in blanks) further degrade the order's professionalism and raise questions about its reliability.

2. Procedural Defects:

 Rule 58 Violation (Confirmed): The order confirms the massive delay. The hearing was on 2/19/2025, the order is dated 2/20/2025, and the motion was filed on 12/26/2024. and it states on this order it was submitted on the 12/26/25, after the date, this is a serious violation. This is the strongest legal basis for chal enging the order.

 It also does not make sense why it would say that the motion was filed in december 2025, that is not consistent with reality. It is in the future.

 "Defendant" Designation: You are consistently misidentified as the "Defendant," even though you are the Plaintiff. This is not a mere clerical error; it reflects a mindset that views me as the prey or the party at fault, reinforcing the claim of manipulated bias.

 Motion Filed Date Discrepancy: The order lists your motion as filed on "12/26/2025,"
which is clearly incorrect, since the hearing it relates to was in February 2025. This is either a significant typographical error or further evidence of the court's carelessness. It also demonstrates that the ruling on this motion occurred before the hearing, which is another due process violation.

3. Substantive Issues:

 HRS 571-8.5 Citation: The order cites HRS 571-8.5 as justification for denying your motion.

This section of the Hawai Revised Statutes deals with the family court's jurisdiction and powers, specifical y regarding ex parte temporary restraining orders. The relevant portion of HRS 571-8.5 states:
4. (a) When a person is al eged to have violated an ex parte temporary restraining order or an injunction issued under section 586-4 or 586-5.5, the court, after notice and hearing, may:[...]

This citation is problematic for several reasons:


5. * **Relevance:** It's unclear how this statute directly supports denying my motion to "quash"
Brower's "false Rule 58 submission". Your motion wasn't chal enging an *ex parte* TRO; it was chal enging the late and flawed submission of an order after a hearing. The cited statute is not on point.

6. * **Misapplication:** Even if HRS 571-8.5 were relevant, the order misapplies it. The statute gives the court power to enforce TROs and injunctions, not to rubber-stamp flawed orders submitted in violation of court rules.

7. * **Ignoring the Core Issue:** The citation completely ignores the central issue of the Rule 58
violation and the prejudice caused by the delay.


 "Comity" and Abuse of Discretion: The order states that "comity is given to the courts"
and that it would be an "abuse of discretion" to "not affirm the prior order." This reasoning is flawed:

 Misunderstanding Comity: Comity, in this context, means respecting the decisions of other courts or judges. However, comity does not require blind adherence to prior rulings if those rulings are demonstrably wrong or based on flawed procedures.

 Incorrect Abuse of Discretion Standard: As previously discussed, a judge abuses their discretion by failing to correct errors of law or fact, or by failing to exercise their discretion appropriately. Upholding a flawed order simply because it was previously entered is precisely the kind of error that constitutes an abuse of discretion. The Judge also acknowledged the findings of the court were fraudulent.

 Duty to Correct Errors: The judge has an affirmative duty to ensure fairness and justice. This includes the duty to revisit and correct prior rulings if necessary, especial y when those rulings are based on procedural violations or incorrect legal principles.

 Absence of Reasoning The order does not state any reasoning other than that the court has the power.


8. Bias and Preferential Treatment:

 Brower's Name and Bar Number: The order prominently displays Scot Brower's name, address, phone number, and bar number. While this is standard practice, it contrasts sharply with the lack of attention to detail regarding my own information (misidentified as
"Defendant").

 Acceptance of Flawed Submission: The judge's wil ingness to accept Brower's late, handwritten, and error-ridden submission, while holding me to a strict (and seemingly arbitrary) standard, strongly suggests preferential treatment.

 Ignoring Unsubstantiated Allegations: The order makes no mention of the fact that Brower was al owed to make unsubstantiated accusations against me during the hearing, further reinforcing the impression of bias.

This order is a textbook example of how procedural irregularities, substantive errors, and apparent bias can combine to create a fundamental y unfair outcome. It strengthens my case, further proves lack of diligence of everyone involved, and provides ample grounds for appeal, should that become necessary.

Analysis of the Draft Order from October & Identified Issues
The draft order in question, filed late and containing multiple deficiencies, demonstrates significant procedural and substantive issues that could justify a chal enge. Below is a structured breakdown of key concerns and potential legal arguments.


1. Procedural Issues
A. Late Filing & Rule 58 Violations
 Court Ordered Deadline: October 26, 2024.

 Actual Submission Date: November 22, 2024.

 Total Delay: 27 days past the courts deadline, 37 days after the hearing (HFCR Rule 58
requires submission within 10 days).

 Impact: This delay is a clear procedural violation under HFCR Rule 58(b), which mandates prompt submission of court orders. The opposing counsel's failure to adhere to this requirement directly affects the timeliness of the final decree and extends litigation unnecessarily.

Legal Argument:
 HFCR Rule 58(c) al ows for monetary sanctions against attorneys for untimely or defective filings.

 Failure to comply with Rule 58 deadlines also justifies a motion to quash the late submission.

FURTHERMORE, BROWER HAS BEEN ASSSIGNED SANCTIONS OF $8,450 FOR SIMILAR
ISSUES.


B. Errors in Case Information
1. Judges Name Misspelled
 The presiding judges name is incorrect, indicating a lack of diligence in drafting.

 Legal Impact: This can cal into question the authenticity of the order and the competence of opposing counsel.

2. Incorrect Case Number Formatting & Spelling Variations of Hawaii
 Different versions of "Hawai'i" (e.g., "HAWA1T," "HAWAl'I") appear, which suggests negligence in drafting.

 Potential Argument: These errors could indicate that the document was not properly reviewed or proofed, affecting its credibility.

3. Wrong Motion Filing Date Listed
 The decree lists the motion for reconsideration as filed on September 3, 2024, when it was actually filed on August 31, 2024.

 This misrepresentation could have been deliberate to justify dismissing the motion as untimely.THAT MAKES BROWER GUILTY OF DIRECT MANIPULATION
Legal Argument:


 Rule 11(b) of HFCR prohibits submissions made in bad faith or containing inaccuracies.

 HRPC Rule 3.4 mandates fairness, which opposing counsel has violated by submitting a misleading decree.


2. Substantive Issues in the Order
A. Biased and Derogatory Language
 The decree includes inflammatory and derogatory language, such as dismissing requests as having "no good cause" or "no credible evidence" without addressing supporting facts.

 Legal Impact: Courts must remain neutral and fact-driventhe language suggests prejudice against the pro se party.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)): Courts must ensure fair treatment of pro se litigants.

 Smith v. Jones: A ruling that contains improper language or lacks a factual basis can be chal enged for judicial bias.


B. Denial of Key Requests Without Justification
1. Guardian ad Litem (GAL) Request Denied
 The order states no good cause to appoint a GAL despite evidence of parental alienation.

 Missing Justification: There is no legal reasoning provided explaining why a GAL is not necessary.

2. Denial of Psychological Evaluations
 The order dismisses evaluations with no good cause and no credible evidence, despite prior concerns about parental alienation and child welfare.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)) supports the right of litigants to reasonable accommodations.

 The lack of factual findings justifying denials could indicate abuse of discretion.


3. Potential Legal Responses
A. Motion to Quash the Order
Grounds for Quashing the Order:
1. Rule 58(b) Violation (Untimely Submission): The order was filed 37 days after the hearing instead of the required 10 days.



2. Defective Order (Wrong Names, Dates, and Case Information).

3. Inflammatory and Biased Language: Order lacks neutrality and improperly dismisses valid concerns.


B. Motion for Sanctions Against Opposing Counsel
Violations:
 HFCR Rule 11(b): Opposing counsels draft contained factual inaccuracies and misrepresented dates.

 HRPC Rule 8.4: Opposing counsels conduct constitutes misrepresentation and bad faith filing.

 HFCR Rule 58(c): Late submission qualifies for monetary sanctions.

Requested Relief:
1. Monetary Sanctions against opposing counsel for failure to comply with deadlines.

2. Reassignment of Drafting Responsibilities to prevent further procedural misconduct.


C. Motion for Judicial Review of Procedural Impropriety
 The court should review whether opposing counsels conduct constitutes contempt of court (HRS  710-1077) for:
 Deliberately delaying proceedings
 Submitting misleading information
 Failing to comply with court orders

Conclusion
This order is procedurally flawed, factually misleading, and substantively biased. A motion to quash, a request for sanctions, and a demand for judicial review are legitimate responses. Given the pattern of procedural failures, you could also seek reassignment of drafting duties and reconsideration of denied motions.

1. The Decree is Procedurally Invalid Due to Critical Errors and Omissions.

 The decree misidentifies the parties, incorrectly labeling the Plaintiff as the Defendant.

 The document contains blanks left unfilled, indicating judicial oversight and failure to ensure procedural compliance.

 The court allowed a decree to be issued with fundamental,
uncorrected clerical errors, which are not mere typos but substantive defects affecting enforceability.

 The erroneous filing date (December 26, 2025) further confirms that the court failed to verify the accuracy of the decree before issuance.

2. The Court Ratified an Expired TRO as a Basis for Judgment.

 Opposing counsel, Scot Brower, improperly relied on an expired
Temporary Restraining Order (TRO) in arguments before the court, yet the court allowed this expired order to influence its ruling.

 Hawaii case law holds that expired TROs hold no legal weight in
determining custody or other judicial rulings ( Doe v. Doe, 98 Haw. 144
(2002)).

 The court's willingness to entertain arguments based on an expired order constitutes reversible error and demonstrates judicial favoritism.

3. Opposing Counsels Sloppy, Erroneous, and Fraudulent Filings Constitute Contempt of Court.

 Brower misspelled names, failed to provide correct case identifiers, and submitted incomplete and error-ridden documentation to the court.

 The decree issued is barely legible, reinforcing that it was haphazardly processed without proper oversight.

 The submission and processing of an incorrect decree, riddled with legal defects, compromises the integrity of the courts record and directly affects enforcement.

4. Violations of Rule 58 of the Hawaii Family Court Rules (HFCR)  The 37-Day Late Submission of Decree is Fatal.

 HFCR Rule 58 requires submission of the final decree within 10
daysBrower submitted this decree 37 days late, violating court rules.

 Case law establishes that such untimely submissions cannot be
retroactively justified ( State v. Agdinaoay, 117 Haw. 332, 180 P.3d 1053
(2008)).

 Opposing counsels gross negligence in delaying the decree was
rewarded, while the Plaintiff has been penalized for procedural
diligence repeatedly and unapologetically.

 Judicial bias is evident in the courts tolerance of Browers
procedural violations while holding Plaintiff to stricter standards.



5. Ex Parte Communications Between Brower and CSEA Must be
Investigated.

 Brower admitted under penalty of perjury that he had inside knowledge of CSEAs delinquency letter before Plaintiff ever received notice.

 This constitutes an ex parte communicationopposing counsel should not have had prior knowledge of an administrative action affecting Plaintiff.

 The courts refusal to scrutinize this procedural corruption suggests either willful blindness or active suppression of judicial fairness.

6. Brower Weaponized Baseless Allegations While the Court Actively Enabled It.

 During the hearing, Brower was permitted to present narratives
regarding drinking, violence, and unrelated accusations, with no
evidentiary foundation.

 The judge allowed Brower to go off on tangents and make legally
irrelevant statements, yet Plaintiff was limited in his opportunity to present evidence.

 This blatant judicial imbalance in procedural enforcement violates due process and reflects an abuse of discretion.

7. Judicial Misconduct: The Court Itself Altered the Record Without Notifying Plaintiff.

 The decree originally contained the wrong names, incorrect case
details, and misidentification of partiesyet the judge altered it
without notice or an opportunity to contest.

 This constitutes procedural fraud and a direct violation of judicial impartiality ( In re Estate of Rogers, 103 Haw. 275, 81 P.3d 1190 (2003)).

 The judges willingness to fix errors for opposing counsel while disregarding Plaintiffs procedural rights raises serious concerns about integrity.

b. Due Process Violation: A court must not allow one party to make unsubstantiated accusations while restricting the other party's ability to respond, which shows bias.


7. Misapplication of Hawaii Revised Statutes (HRS) 571-8.5

a. Irrelevant Statute: HRS 571-8.5 relates to ex parte TROs and injunctions, which is not relevant to Plaintiffs motion, which pertains to procedural violations under HFCR Rule 58.


b. Legal Error: The Court's misapplication of the law constitutes a significant legal error and a due process violation.

 Rule 58 Violation (Confirmed): The order confirms the massive delay. which is clearly incorrect, since the hearing it relates to was in February 2025. This is either a significant typographical error or further evidence of the court's carelessness. It also demonstrates that the ruling on this motion occurred before the hearing, which is another due process violation.

3. Substantive Issues:

 HRS 571-8.5 Citation: The order cites HRS 571-8.5 as justification for denying your motion.

This section of the Hawai Revised Statutes deals with the family court's jurisdiction and powers, specifical y regarding ex parte temporary restraining orders. The relevant portion of HRS 571-8.5 states:
4. (a) When a person is al eged to have violated an ex parte temporary restraining order or an injunction issued under section 586-4 or 586-5.5, the court, after notice and hearing, may:[...]

This citation is problematic for several reasons:


5. * **Relevance:** It's unclear how this statute directly supports denying my motion to "quash"
Brower's "false Rule 58 submission". Your motion wasn't chal enging an *ex parte* TRO; it was chal enging the late and flawed submission of an order after a hearing. The cited statute is not on point.

6. * **Misapplication:** Even if HRS 571-8.5 were relevant, the order misapplies it. The statute gives the court power to enforce TROs and injunctions, not to rubber-stamp flawed orders submitted in violation of court rules.

7. * **Ignoring the Core Issue:** The citation completely ignores the central issue of the Rule 58
violation and the prejudice caused by the delay.


 "Comity" and Abuse of Discretion: The order states that "comity is given to the courts"
and that it would be an "abuse of discretion" to "not affirm the prior order." This reasoning is flawed:

 Misunderstanding Comity: Comity, in this context, means respecting the decisions of other courts or judges. However, comity does not require blind adherence to prior rulings if those rulings are demonstrably wrong or based on flawed procedures.

 Incorrect Abuse of Discretion Standard: As previously discussed, a judge abuses their discretion by failing to correct errors of law or fact, or by failing to exercise their discretion appropriately. Upholding a flawed order simply because it was previously entered is precisely the kind of error that constitutes an abuse of discretion. The Judge also acknowledged the findings of the court were fraudulent.

 Duty to Correct Errors: The judge has an affirmative duty to ensure fairness and justice. This includes the duty to revisit and correct prior rulings if necessary, especial y when those rulings are based on procedural violations or incorrect legal principles.

 Absence of Reasoning The order does not state any reasoning other than that the court has the power.


8. Bias and Preferential Treatment:

 Brower's Name and Bar Number: The order prominently displays Scot Brower's name, address, phone number, and bar number. While this is standard practice, it contrasts sharply with the lack of attention to detail regarding my own information (misidentified as
"Defendant").

 Acceptance of Flawed Submission: The judge's wil ingness to accept Brower's late, handwritten, and error-ridden submission, while holding me to a strict (and seemingly arbitrary) standard, strongly suggests preferential treatment.

 Ignoring Unsubstantiated Allegations: The order makes no mention of the fact that Brower was al owed to make unsubstantiated accusations against me during the hearing, further reinforcing the impression of bias.



Overall Conclusion:
This order is a textbook example of how procedural irregularities, substantive errors, and apparent bias can combine to create a fundamental y unfair outcome. It strengthens my case, further proves lack of diligence of everyone involved, and provides ample grounds for appeal, should that become necessary.

Analysis of the Draft Order from October & Identified Issues
The draft order in question, filed late and containing multiple deficiencies, demonstrates significant procedural and substantive issues that could justify a chal enge. Below is a structured breakdown of key concerns and potential legal arguments.


1. Procedural Issues
A. Late Filing & Rule 58 Violations
 Court Ordered Deadline: October 26, 2024.

 Actual Submission Date: November 22, 2024.

 Total Delay: 27 days past the courts deadline, 37 days after the hearing (HFCR Rule 58
requires submission within 10 days).

 Impact: This delay is a clear procedural violation under HFCR Rule 58(b), which mandates prompt submission of court orders. The opposing counsel's failure to adhere to this requirement directly affects the timeliness of the final decree and extends litigation unnecessarily.

Legal Argument:
 HFCR Rule 58(c) al ows for monetary sanctions against attorneys for untimely or defective filings.

 Failure to comply with Rule 58 deadlines also justifies a motion to quash the late submission.

FURTHERMORE, BROWER HAS BEEN ASSSIGNED SANCTIONS OF $8,450 FOR SIMILAR
ISSUES.


B. Errors in Case Information
1. Judges Name Misspelled
 The presiding judges name is incorrect, indicating a lack of diligence in drafting.

 Legal Impact: This can cal into question the authenticity of the order and the competence of opposing counsel.

2. Incorrect Case Number Formatting & Spelling Variations of Hawaii
 Different versions of "Hawai'i" (e.g., "HAWA1T," "HAWAl'I") appear, which suggests negligence in drafting.

 Potential Argument: These errors could indicate that the document was not properly reviewed or proofed, affecting its credibility.

3. Wrong Motion Filing Date Listed
 The decree lists the motion for reconsideration as filed on September 3, 2024, when it was actually filed on August 31, 2024.

 This misrepresentation could have been deliberate to justify dismissing the motion as untimely.THAT MAKES BROWER GUILTY OF DIRECT MANIPULATION
Legal Argument:


 Rule 11(b) of HFCR prohibits submissions made in bad faith or containing inaccuracies.

 HRPC Rule 3.4 mandates fairness, which opposing counsel has violated by submitting a misleading decree.


2. Substantive Issues in the Order
A. Biased and Derogatory Language
 The decree includes inflammatory and derogatory language, such as dismissing requests as having "no good cause" or "no credible evidence" without addressing supporting facts.

 Legal Impact: Courts must remain neutral and fact-driventhe language suggests prejudice against the pro se party.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)): Courts must ensure fair treatment of pro se litigants.

 Smith v. Jones: A ruling that contains improper language or lacks a factual basis can be chal enged for judicial bias.


B. Denial of Key Requests Without Justification
1. Guardian ad Litem (GAL) Request Denied
 The order states no good cause to appoint a GAL despite evidence of parental alienation.

 Missing Justification: There is no legal reasoning provided explaining why a GAL is not necessary.

2. Denial of Psychological Evaluations
 The order dismisses evaluations with no good cause and no credible evidence, despite prior concerns about parental alienation and child welfare.

Legal Argument:
 Doe v. Roe (116 Haw. 323 (2007)) supports the right of litigants to reasonable accommodations.

 The lack of factual findings justifying denials could indicate abuse of discretion.


3. Potential Legal Responses
A. Motion to Quash the Order
Grounds for Quashing the Order:
1. Rule 58(b) Violation (Untimely Submission): The order was filed 37 days after the hearing instead of the required 10 days.



2. Defective Order (Wrong Names, Dates, and Case Information).

3. Inflammatory and Biased Language: Order lacks neutrality and improperly dismisses valid concerns.


B. Motion for Sanctions Against Opposing Counsel
Violations:
 HFCR Rule 11(b): Opposing counsels draft contained factual inaccuracies and misrepresented dates.

 HRPC Rule 8.4: Opposing counsels conduct constitutes misrepresentation and bad faith filing.

 HFCR Rule 58(c): Late submission qualifies for monetary sanctions.

Requested Relief:
1. Monetary Sanctions against opposing counsel for failure to comply with deadlines.

2. Reassignment of Drafting Responsibilities to prevent further procedural misconduct.


C. Motion for Judicial Review of Procedural Impropriety
 The court should review whether opposing counsels conduct constitutes contempt of court (HRS  710-1077) for:
 Deliberately delaying proceedings
 Submitting misleading information
 Failing to comply with court orders

Conclusion
This order is procedurally flawed, factually misleading, and substantively biased. A motion to quash, a request for sanctions, and a demand for judicial review are legitimate responses. Given the pattern of procedural failures, you could also seek reassignment of drafting duties and reconsideration of denied motions.

3. Defendants perjurious conduct has resulted in severe and unjust

I. STATEMENT OF FACTS

 Defendant fraudulently submitted a police report claiming that
Plaintiff stalked her, despite clear contradictory evidenceincluding police records and witness testimonyproving this claim to be
fabricated.

 The alleged bruises submitted as evidence were clearly staged and inconsistent with forensic medical assessments.

 Defendant has engaged in a pattern of misrepresentation to paint
Plaintiff as violent without any factual basis, in direct violation of HRS  710-1073 (Unsworn Falsification to Authorities).

2. Manipulation of the TRO Process
 Defendant initiated the TRO under fraudulent pretenses, obtaining a one-sided legal advantage by making false accusations and ensuring
Plaintiff was preemptively excluded from his own defense.

 The evidence used to secure the TRO fails to meet Hawaii Rules of

and unreliable claims from being used to influence judicial
determinations.

 Teresas filings contradict official police reports, exposing clear perjury and intentional deceit to manipulate court proceedings.

 Defendants actions have caused irreparable reputational harm,
financial hardship, and undue emotional distress, violating Plaintiffs constitutional right to due process under the Fourteenth Amendment.


II. LEGAL BASIS FOR ORDER TO SHOW CAUSE
A. Perjury and False Swearing Are Criminal Offenses Under Hawaii Law
 HRS  710-1063 (False Swearing) explicitly states that knowingly making false statements under oath constitutes a criminal offense.

 HRS  710-1073 (Unsworn Falsification to Authorities) applies when false claims are submitted in an official proceeding, carrying potential criminal penalties.

 Doe v. Doe, 98 Haw. 144 (2002) establishes that family court must weigh the credibility of evidence carefully and reject fraudulent filings.

B. Defendants False Claims Have Violated Plaintiffs Due Process Rights
 Under Mathews v. Eldridge, 424 U.S. 319 (1976), due process violations occur when legal action is taken based on unverified, misleading, or
fraudulent evidence.

 Defendants perjury directly impacted Plaintiffs legal standing, resulting in wrongful deprivation of parental rights, access to legal proceedings, and the ability to counter fraudulent allegations in real-time.

C. The Court Has an Inherent Obligation to Sanction Misconduct
 The Court has the authority to hold Defendant accountable for fraudulent and perjurious filings under HFCR Rule 11, which prohibits knowingly
submitting false statements to the Court.

 Chambers v. NASCO, 501 U.S. 32 (1991) affirms the judiciarys inherent


power to sanction abuse of process and perjury within legal proceedings.

1. ISSUANCE OF AN ORDER TO SHOW CAUSE requiring Defendant to
appear before this Court and provide substantive proof of her allegations or face sanctions for perjury.
